        </script><script type="text/javascript">window.cns = window.cns || {}; window.cns.pageContext = {"channel":"reviews","content":{"copyCount":699,"imageCount":0,"embedCount":0,"ratio":0,"midContentCadence":500},"contentType":"article","experiments":{"bucketLabel":[],"experimentLabel":[],"payload":[]},"functionalTags":["opinion"],"keywords":{"copilotid":["696a954e57b861f79f535be5"],"platform":["verso"],"tags":["reviews","albums"]},"targeting":{"feature_flags":[]},"server":"production","slug":"asap-rocky-dont-be-dumb","subChannel":"albums","subSubChannel":"","templateType":"mt_review"};</script><link rel="preconnect" href="https://ads-static.conde.digital"/><script id="ads-v6" src="https://ads-static.conde.digital/production/cns/builds/pitchfork/v6.js?v=12094" async=""></script><script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"http://schema.org","@type":"Review","alternativeHeadline":"Rocky’s fourth album is flawed but sharp, and contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that he’s still an essential part of the rap ecosystem.","keywords":["albums","web"],"thumbnailUrl":"https://media.pitchfork.com/photos/696a9716019618f4a187ec3d/2:3/w_666,h_999,c_limit/ASAP%20Rocky%20-%20Don't%20Be%20Dumb%20cover%20art.jpg","publisher":{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"Organization","name":"Pitchfork","logo":{"@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://pitchfork.com/verso/static/pitchfork-us/assets/logo-seo.png","width":"565px","height":"100px"},"url":"https://pitchfork.com"},"isPartOf":{"@type":"CreativeWork","name":"Pitchfork"},"isAccessibleForFree":true,"author":[{"@type":"Person","name":"Paul A. Thompson","sameAs":"https://pitchfork.com/staff/paul-a. thompson/"}],"image":{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://media.pitchfork.com/photos/696a9716019618f4a187ec3d/2:3/w_666,h_999,c_limit/ASAP%20Rocky%20-%20Don't%20Be%20Dumb%20cover%20art.jpg","height":1000,"width":1000},"description":"Rocky’s fourth album is flawed but sharp, and contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that he’s still an essential part of the rap ecosystem.","itemReviewed":{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"MusicRecording","name":"A$AP Rocky: Don’t Be Dumb","description":"Rocky’s fourth album is flawed but sharp, and contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that he’s still an essential part of the rap ecosystem.","image":{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"ImageObject","url":"https://media.pitchfork.com/photos/696a9716019618f4a187ec3d/2:3/w_666,h_999,c_limit/ASAP%20Rocky%20-%20Don't%20Be%20Dumb%20cover%20art.jpg","height":1000,"width":1000},"brand":{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"Brand"}},"articleSection":"reviews","url":"https://pitchfork.com/reviews/albums/asap-rocky-dont-be-dumb/","headline":"Don’t Be Dumb","reviewBody":"For all the conspicuously expensive samples, the breathless reporting about a $3 million record deal, the Vogue shoots, the white women muttering slurs, the Spike Lee movies, the guns, the kids, the feuds with superstars, and the overtures from a president, A$AP Rocky has always been best when stripped down to core principles. The invocations of Houston rap on “Purple Swag” are secondary to how deliberate and direct Rocky is; this was true of “Peso,” of “Goldie,” of everything that made him click. All the supposed post-regionalism—the borrowing from Houston and Memphis and the jagged online underground—was simply the medium through which a new Harlem superstar had chosen to express himself, like Big L’s glossy hypertechnique or Cam’ron’s Giuliani-baiting absurdity.\nDon’t Be Dumb, Rocky’s first album in almost eight years, arrives at a time and in a manner that seems to be staked entirely on celebrity meta-commentary. Yet while his media circus of a 2025 trial provided something of an organizing structure (last February, Rocky was acquitted in Los Angeles on two counts of assault with a semiautomatic firearm, a felony for which he potentially faced more than 20 years in prison), and while he includes a mostly tepid response to Drake’s “Family Matters,” Rocky largely uses his fourth LP to narrow his focus. Though poorly sequenced and littered with duds, Don’t Be Dumb contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that Rocky is still an essential part of the rap ecosystem\nIn some ways, the “almost eight years” timeline is misleading: The last Rocky album that felt like an event was 2015’s At.Long.Last.A$AP. Testing, his record from three years later, failed to make the same impact on the charts or in the world as all his work that came before. But while it’s derailed almost immediately by its second song, the T.I.-, Kid Cudi-, and Moby-featuring “A$AP Forever” remix—a near-perfect encapsulation of the baroque overproduction and preciousness that often drowned Rocky on 2013’s Long.Live.A$AP—Testing later reveals some of the rapper’s most interesting instincts. Songs like “Tony Tone,” “Praise the Lord (Da Shine),” “OG Beeper,” and “Hun43rd” are minimal, strange, and occasionally intricate—all staked on a vocal control that had emerged in the A.L.L.A. sessions but was not foregrounded on that album. For those who hung around long enough to hear it, the middle of Testing exhibits an A$AP Rocky who is no longer content to be just one instrument among many.\nDon’t Be Dumb is not exactly Rocky’s rage-rap album, but the production is certainly informed by the quicker tempos that have come into vogue during his hiatus. The mix on early single “Helicopter” is busy, but the song is sharp and unidirectional; the album’s best beats (Kelvin Krash, KayCyy, and SpaceGhostPurrp’s “Swat Team,” and Rocky’s own “No Trespassing” and “Air Force (Black Demarco)”) are defined by palpitating drums and a relentless forward motion. These underline—and in some cases are only navigable due to—that new vocal acuity. It’s difficult to imagine the Rocky from Live.Love.A$AP moving between modes as fluidly as he does on “Air Force,” or the Rocky from Long.Live being confident enough to treat his vocals like the song’s anchor.\nUnfortunately, the energy flags: “Punk Rocky” is trite, the jazzy “Robbery” is hampered by an over-theatrical Doechii, and the Drake diss “Stole Ya Flow” is somehow emptier than Drake’s non-insults (You’re beautiful, I love your wife) from nearly two years ago. (The flaccidity of “Stole Ya Flow” is mitigated somewhat by Rocky’s inclusion of a truly show-stopping verse from Sauce Walka on “Stop Snitching,” author of one of the most scathing Drake disses ever.) But before too long, these blemishes are balanced out by irresistibly low-stakes records like “Whiskey (Release Me),” which makes as intuitive a use of Tennesseean triplicate flow as Rocky has since 2011. Don’t Be Dumb is not a blockbuster, and it’s all the better for it.","dateModified":"2026-01-20T00:02:00.000-05:00","datePublished":"2026-01-20T00:02:00.000-05:00"}</script><script id="martech-lib-pre-script">window.Martech = window.Martech || new Promise((resolve) => {

        function extractExperimentData(experiment, result) {
            return {
                experiment_id: experiment.key,
                feature_id: result.featureId || '',
                variation: result.key,
                variation_name: result.name || '',
                payload: result.value

        function updateDataLayer(expDataLayer) {
            if (!Array.isArray(window.dataLayer) ||
                typeof window.dataLayer[0] !== 'object' ||
                expDataLayer.length === 0) {
                return;
            }
            const mergeAssignments = (newAssignments = [], existingAssignments = []) => {
                const merged = [...existingAssignments];
                newAssignments.forEach((newAssignment) => {
                    const existingIndex = merged.findIndex((existing) => existing.experiment_id === newAssignment.experiment_id);
                    if (existingIndex >= 0) {
                        merged[existingIndex] = newAssignment;
                    }
                    else {
                        merged.push(newAssignment);
                    }
                });
                return merged;
            };
            window.dataLayer[0].experiments = mergeAssignments(expDataLayer, window.dataLayer[0].experiments || []);

            }
        }
        function formatExperimentsForPageContext(experiments) {
            if (!experiments.length) {
                return {};
            }
            const bucketLabel = [];
            const experimentLabel = [];
            const payloadArray = [];
            experiments.forEach((experiment) => {
                const { variation_name, experiment_id, payload } = experiment;
                bucketLabel.push(`${variation_name}`);
                experimentLabel.push(`${experiment_id}`);
                if (Array.isArray(payload)) {
                    payloadArray.push(...payload);
                }
                else if (payload) {
                    payloadArray.push(payload);
                }
            });
            return {
                bucketLabel,
                experimentLabel,
                payload: payloadArray
            };
        }
        function perfMark(name) {
            if (typeof window.performance !== 'undefined' &&
                typeof window.performance.mark !== 'undefined') {
                window.performance.mark(name);
            }
        }
        function perfMeasure(name, startMark, endMark) {
            if (typeof window.performance !== 'undefined' &&
                typeof window.performance.measure !== 'undefined') {
                try {
                    window.performance.measure(name, startMark, endMark);
                }
                catch (e) {
                    console.error(`Error setting performance measure: ${e}`);

            }
        }
        function getCookie(name) {
            if (typeof document === 'undefined' || !name) {
                return '';
            }
            const cookiePieces = document.cookie.split(/;\s?/);
            let cookieValue = '';
            for (let i = 0; i < cookiePieces.length; i++) {
                const parts = cookiePieces[i].split('=');
                if (parts[0] === name) {
                    cookieValue = decodeURIComponent(parts.slice(1).join('='));
                    break;
                }
            }
            return cookieValue;
        }

            window.growthbook_config.trackingCallback = (experiment, result) => {
                const expDataLayer = [];

                const currentExp = {

                    status: 'CLIENT_ASSIGNMENT'

                expDataLayer.push(currentExp);
                updateDataLayer(expDataLayer);
                perfMark(`gb-script-tracking-end`);
                perfMeasure(`gb-script-tracking-finish`, `gb-script-end`, `gb-script-tracking-end`);
            };
            window.growthbook_queue = window.growthbook_queue || [];
            window.growthbook_queue.push((gb) => {
                const checkReady = () => {
                    if (gb.ready) {
                        perfMark(`gb-script-end`);
                        perfMeasure(`gb-script-ready`, `gb-script-start`, `gb-script-end`);
                    }

SplitScreenContentHeaderByline-kAWXxZ brwskr"><span class="BylineWrapper-jRoBEm hotajz byline bylines__byline" data-testid="BylineWrapper"><span class="BylineNamesWrapper-jrdaOa fXeqQN"><span class="BaseText-eqOrNE SplitScreenContentHeaderDekDown-cnAPJw fLJFjG mkOxZ">Rocky’s fourth album is flawed but sharp, and contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that he’s still an essential part of the rap ecosystem.</div></div></div></div></div><div data-testid="action-bar-wrapper" class="ActionBarWrapperContent-kKWNzd fMxerH"><div class="ActionBarWrapperComponent-bUaKab eLQkJh"><div data-attr-viewport-monitor="" class="ActionBarWrapper-dwTaBY cDLkyz viewport-monitor-anchor"><button id="bookmark" aria-label="Save this story" class="ActionBarButton-dDYFhp foyWVZ bookmark large-screen"><span class="ActionBarSecondaryButtonPrimaryIcon-iHxjkE jHLNtO bookmark-button-icon"><svg class="icon icon-bookmark" width="24" height="24" fill="none" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg"><title>Save Story</title><path d="M11.6279 17.2126L5 21V4H19V21L12.3721 17.2126L12 17L11.6279 17.2126Z" stroke="black" stroke-width="1.5" stroke-linecap="square" data-attr-viewport-monitor="" class="ActionBarWrapper-dwTaBY lcNJMY viewport-monitor-anchor"><button id="bookmark" aria-label="Save this story" class="ActionBarButton-dDYFhp caOZoN bookmark mobile"><span class="ActionBarSecondaryButtonPrimaryIcon-iHxjkE jHLNtO bookmark-button-icon"><svg class="icon icon-bookmark" width="24" height="24" fill="none" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg"><title>Save Story</title><path d="M11.6279 17.2126L5 21V4H19V21L12.3721 17.2126L12 17L11.6279 17.2126Z" stroke="black" stroke-width="1.5" stroke-linecap="square" class="icon-bookmark-stroke" fill="none"></path></svg></span><span class="BaseText-eqOrNE ActionBarButtonText-bTFimM kDNmfr eJRZYB">Save this story</span></button></div></div></div><div class="GridWrapper-cFSKbf kfCuhH grid grid-margins grid-items-2 grid-layout--adrail narrow" data-journey-hook="grid-wrapper"><div class="GridItem-beYvyV kCPYUp grid--item grid-layout__content"><div data-testid="ReviewPageMainContent"></div></div></div><div class="ArticlePageChunks-fwcPjP gRwuHP"><div class="GridWrapper-cFSKbf kfCuhH grid grid-margins grid-items-2 grid-layout--adrail narrow" data-journey-hook="grid-wrapper"><div class="GridItem-beYvyV kCPYUp grid--item grid-layout__content"><div class="BodyWrapper-kzyFNv bpYdXB body body__container article__body" data-testid="BodyWrapper"><div class="body__inner-container"><div class="Container-VgtPr ekoLDG"></div><p>For all the conspicuously expensive samples, the breathless reporting about a $3 million record deal, the <em>Vogue</em> shoots, <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KuZ2QZKYj7c">the white women muttering slurs</a>, the Spike Lee movies, the guns, the kids, the feuds with superstars, and the <a data-offer-url="https://www.rollingstone.com/music/music-news/asap-rocky-trump-sweden-movie-1183705/" class="external-link" data-event-click="{&quot;element&quot;:&quot;ExternalLink&quot;,&quot;outgoingURL&quot;:&quot;https://www.rollingstone.com/music/music-news/asap-rocky-trump-sweden-movie-1183705/&quot;}" href="https://www.rollingstone.com/music/music-news/asap-rocky-trump-sweden-movie-1183705/" rel="nofollow noopener" target="_blank">overtures from a president</a>, <a href="https://pitchfork.com/artists/30007-asap-rocky/">A$AP Rocky</a> has always been best when stripped down to core principles. The invocations of Houston rap on “Purple Swag” are secondary to how deliberate and direct Rocky is; this was true of “<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ob3ktDxAjWI&amp;list=RDob3ktDxAjWI&amp;start_radio=1">Peso</a>,” of “<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fLCf-URqIf0&amp;list=RDfLCf-URqIf0&amp;start_radio=1">Goldie</a>,” of everything that made him click. All the supposed post-regionalism—the borrowing from Houston and Memphis and the jagged online underground—was simply the medium through which a new Harlem superstar had chosen to express himself, like Big L’s glossy hypertechnique or Cam’ron’s Giuliani-baiting absurdity.</p><p><em>Don’t Be Dumb</em>, Rocky’s first album in almost eight years, arrives at a time and in a manner that seems to be staked entirely on celebrity meta-commentary. Yet while his media circus of a 2025 trial provided something of an organizing structure (last February, <a href="https://pitchfork.com/news/adollarap-rocky-found-not-guilty-in-alleged-shooting-of-former-friend/">Rocky was acquitted in Los Angeles</a> on two counts of assault with a semiautomatic firearm, a felony for which he potentially faced more than 20 years in prison), and while he includes a mostly tepid response to <a href="https://pitchfork.com/artists/27950-drake/">Drake</a>’s “<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZkXG3ZrXlbc">Family Matters</a>,” Rocky largely uses his fourth LP to narrow his focus. Though poorly sequenced and littered with duds, <em>Don’t Be Dumb</em> contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that Rocky is still an essential part of the rap ecosystem</p><div class="ListenerScoreWrapper-bXjOXJ ihJjsp" data-testid="ListenerScore"><div data-testid="ListenerScoreHeader" class="ListenerScoreHeader-fJWTJp cRcyhQ">Reader Score</div><div class="ListenerScoreTextWrapper-hCvjNU fatKeo"><div class="ListenerScoreTextContainer-dAAlDQ hmJZtm"><p data-testid="ListenerScoreNoScoreText" class="BaseText-eqOrNE ListenerScoreThresholdText-hWTiXA jKEMra hQevxL">No score yet, be the first to add.</p></div><div class="ListenerScoreCircularWrapper-jzJcqM biSjwa"><div class="CircularRatingWrapper-bwAyix bPvJFC undefined blurred"><div class="CircularRatingCircle-dzyxfI bGbTHh"><p class="BaseText-eqOrNE CircularRatingNumber-exsFsV fStfTD gaOXvA">0.0</p></div></div></div></div></div><p>In some ways, the “almost eight years” timeline is misleading: The last Rocky album that felt like an event was 2015’s <a href="https://pitchfork.com/reviews/albums/20661-atlonglastaap/"><em>At.Long.Last.A$AP</em></a>. <a href="https://pitchfork.com/reviews/albums/asap-rocky-testing/"><em>Testing</em></a>, his record from three years later, failed to make the same impact on the charts or in the world as all his work that came before. But while it’s derailed almost immediately by its second song, the <a href="https://pitchfork.com/artists/4400-ti/">T.I.</a>-, <a href="https://pitchfork.com/artists/8562-kid-cudi/">Kid Cudi</a>-, and <a href="https://pitchfork.com/artists/2795-moby/">Moby</a>-featuring “<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=qxUKNrzX20M">A$AP Forever</a>” remix—a near-perfect encapsulation of the baroque overproduction and preciousness that often drowned Rocky on 2013’s <a href="https://pitchfork.com/reviews/albums/17505-longliveaap/"><em>Long.Live.A$AP</em></a>—<em>Testing</em> later reveals some of the rapper’s most interesting instincts. Songs like “Tony Tone,” “Praise the Lord (Da Shine),” “OG Beeper,” and “Hun43rd” are minimal, strange, and occasionally intricate—all staked on a vocal control that had emerged in the <em>A.L.L.A.</em> sessions but was not foregrounded on that album. For those who hung around long enough to hear it, the middle of <em>Testing</em> exhibits an A$AP Rocky who is no longer content to be just one instrument among many.</p><native-ad position="in-content" shouldDisplayLabel="true"></native-ad><p><em>Don’t Be Dumb</em> is not exactly Rocky’s rage-rap album, but the production is certainly informed by the quicker tempos that have come into vogue during his hiatus. The mix on early single “Helicopter” is busy, but the song is sharp and unidirectional; the album’s best beats (Kelvin Krash, KayCyy, and SpaceGhostPurrp’s “Swat Team,” and Rocky’s own “No Trespassing” and “Air Force (Black Demarco)”) are defined by palpitating drums and a relentless forward motion. These underline—and in some cases are only navigable due to—that new vocal acuity. It’s difficult to imagine the Rocky from <em>Live.Love.A$AP</em> moving between modes as fluidly as he does on “Air Force,” or the Rocky from <em>Long.Live</em> being confident enough to treat his vocals like the song’s anchor.</p><p>Unfortunately, the energy flags: “Punk Rocky” is trite, the jazzy “Robbery” is hampered by an over-theatrical <a href="https://pitchfork.com/reviews/albums/doechii-alligator-bites-never-heal/">Doechii</a>, and the Drake diss “Stole Ya Flow” is somehow emptier than Drake’s non-insults (<em>You’re beautiful</em>, <em>I love your wife</em>) from nearly two years ago. (The flaccidity of “Stole Ya Flow” is mitigated somewhat by Rocky’s inclusion of a truly show-stopping verse from Sauce Walka on “Stop Snitching,” author of one of the most <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mxQwRX8ZWP0">scathing Drake disses</a> ever.) But before too long, these blemishes are balanced out by irresistibly low-stakes records like “Whiskey (Release Me),” which makes as intuitive a use of Tennesseean triplicate flow as Rocky has since 2011. <em>Don’t Be Dumb</em> is not a blockbuster, and it’s all the better for it.</p></div></div></div><div class="GridItem-beYvyV dUPlfX grid--item grid-layout__aside"><div SummaryItemResponsiveAsset-gUkUUp cDwxAB summary-item__image responsive-image"><noscript><source media="(max-width: 767px)" Thompson","sameAs":"https:\u002F\u002Fpitchfork.com\u002Fstaff\u002Fpaul-a. thompson\u002F"}],"image":{"@context":"https:\u002F\u002Fschema.org","@type":"ImageObject","url":"https:\u002F\u002Fmedia.pitchfork.com\u002Fphotos\u002F696a9716019618f4a187ec3d\u002F2:3\u002Fw_666,h_999,c_limit\u002FASAP%20Rocky%20-%20Don't%20Be%20Dumb%20cover%20art.jpg","height":1000,"width":1000},"description":"Rocky’s fourth album is flawed but sharp, and contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that he’s still an essential part of the rap ecosystem.","itemReviewed":{"@context":"https:\u002F\u002Fschema.org","@type":"MusicRecording","name":"A$AP Rocky: Don’t Be Dumb","description":"Rocky’s fourth album is flawed but sharp, and contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that he’s still an essential part of the rap ecosystem.","image":{"@context":"https:\u002F\u002Fschema.org","@type":"ImageObject","url":"https:\u002F\u002Fmedia.pitchfork.com\u002Fphotos\u002F696a9716019618f4a187ec3d\u002F2:3\u002Fw_666,h_999,c_limit\u002FASAP%20Rocky%20-%20Don't%20Be%20Dumb%20cover%20art.jpg","height":1000,"width":1000},"brand":{"@context":"https:\u002F\u002Fschema.org","@type":"Brand"}},"articleSection":"reviews","url":"https:\u002F\u002Fpitchfork.com\u002Freviews\u002Falbums\u002Fasap-rocky-dont-be-dumb\u002F","headline":"Don’t Be Dumb","reviewBody":"For all the conspicuously expensive samples, the breathless reporting about a $3 million record deal, the Vogue shoots, the white women muttering slurs, the Spike Lee movies, the guns, the kids, the feuds with superstars, and the overtures from a president, A$AP Rocky has always been best when stripped down to core principles. The invocations of Houston rap on “Purple Swag” are secondary to how deliberate and direct Rocky is; this was true of “Peso,” of “Goldie,” of everything that made him click. All the supposed post-regionalism—the borrowing from Houston and Memphis and the jagged online underground—was simply the medium through which a new Harlem superstar had chosen to express himself, like Big L’s glossy hypertechnique or Cam’ron’s Giuliani-baiting absurdity.\nDon’t Be Dumb, Rocky’s first album in almost eight years, arrives at a time and in a manner that seems to be staked entirely on celebrity meta-commentary. Yet while his media circus of a 2025 trial provided something of an organizing structure (last February, Rocky was acquitted in Los Angeles on two counts of assault with a semiautomatic firearm, a felony for which he potentially faced more than 20 years in prison), and while he includes a mostly tepid response to Drake’s “Family Matters,” Rocky largely uses his fourth LP to narrow his focus. Though poorly sequenced and littered with duds, Don’t Be Dumb contains enough moments of ingenuity to argue that Rocky is still an essential part of the rap ecosystem\nIn some ways, the “almost eight years” timeline is misleading: The last Rocky album that felt like an event was 2015’s At.Long.Last.A$AP. Testing, his record from three years later, failed to make the same impact on the charts or in the world as all his work that came before. But while it’s derailed almost immediately by its second song, the T.I.-, Kid Cudi-, and Moby-featuring “A$AP Forever” remix—a near-perfect encapsulation of the baroque overproduction and preciousness that often drowned Rocky on 2013’s Long.Live.A$AP—Testing later reveals some of the rapper’s most interesting instincts. Songs like “Tony Tone,” “Praise the Lord (Da Shine),” “OG Beeper,” and “Hun43rd” are minimal, strange, and occasionally intricate—all staked on a vocal control that had emerged in the A.L.L.A. sessions but was not foregrounded on that album. For those who hung around long enough to hear it, the middle of Testing exhibits an A$AP Rocky who is no longer content to be just one instrument among many.\nDon’t Be Dumb is not exactly Rocky’s rage-rap album, but the production is certainly informed by the quicker tempos that have come into vogue during his hiatus. The mix on early single “Helicopter” is busy, but the song is sharp and unidirectional; the album’s best beats (Kelvin Krash, KayCyy, and SpaceGhostPurrp’s “Swat Team,” and Rocky’s own “No Trespassing” and “Air Force (Black Demarco)”) are defined by palpitating drums and a relentless forward motion. These underline—and in some cases are only navigable due to—that new vocal acuity. It’s difficult to imagine the Rocky from Live.Love.A$AP moving between modes as fluidly as he does on “Air Force,” or the Rocky from Long.Live being confident enough to treat his vocals like the song’s anchor.\nUnfortunately, the energy flags: “Punk Rocky” is trite, the jazzy “Robbery” is hampered by an over-theatrical Doechii, and the Drake diss “Stole Ya Flow” is somehow emptier than Drake’s non-insults (You’re beautiful, I love your wife) from nearly two years ago. (The flaccidity of “Stole Ya Flow” is mitigated somewhat by Rocky’s inclusion of a truly show-stopping verse from Sauce Walka on “Stop Snitching,” author of one of the most scathing Drake disses ever.) But before too long, these blemishes are balanced out by irresistibly low-stakes records like “Whiskey (Release Me),” which makes as intuitive a use of Tennesseean triplicate flow as Rocky has since 2011. Don’t Be Dumb is not a blockbuster, and it’s all the better for
